0062014536|3.0|I have been waiting impatiently for this book to come out since I finished Pandemonium.  This series has been one of my favorites, but I feel like this concluding book falls short of the previous two.  In the first two books the world felt so much richer and the characters more real.  In Requiem I felt like the characters lost a lot of their appeal.  I couldn't remember why I loved Alex in Delirium or why I loved Julian in Pandemonium.  They were so boring.  Both of the boys took a back seat to all of this revolution business which was not even tied off nicely in the end.  Lena also didn't seem to have the depth she had in the previous books.  I felt like she didn't really feel anything.  She seemed indifferent towards both Julian and Alex for the most part, when previously she felt so many emotions towards them.  There was none of the romance that makes your heart flutter like there was in the first two books.  In a world where you are fighting for the right to love, I expected a lot more focus on the love triangle than the revolution (which I would have been happy had it not been so heavily focused on).  I didn't really feel like Lena was fighting to love either Julian or Alex.<br /><br />SPOILERS BELOW HERE<br /><br />I felt like the ending was abrupt and messy.  Raven was killed off in such an uneventful way that you really don't notice or care that she is gone (she's not really a big character anymore) and you don't find out what happens to Tack.  In the end Lena chooses Alex.  You get some reasons for this, but its obvious that she still has feelings for Julian.  Also, you don't get to find out how Lena's choice affects Julian and if he ends up happy or not.<br /><br />Half of the book was about Hana and the ending to her story was very bland.  She's wandering about and is like \\"Oh, everything will okay!\\"  I was expecting her to join the Resistance as the Cure didn't seem to work completely on her.  I felt like the old Hana was in there and thought she was going to come out, but she never did.  It seemed like the author couldn't decide whether she wanted Hana to be completely cured or if she wanted the cure to not have worked on her.  Hana did a lot of things that it doesn't seem like a cured person would do - bringing Grace food, lying, sneaking into the Crypts to see Cassie, etc.  She seemed like old Hana most of the time, but then the author would suddenly remember \\"Oh right, she's Cured\\" and she'd have some moments where she acted the way you'd expect a cured person to.  Her character was very inconsistent - at times vibrant and rebellious other times bland.<br /><br />The outcome of the Resistance as a whole was poorly resolved.  So, Portland is freed, but what about the rest of the country?  What's to stop the government from sending troops to take the city back?  The Invalids have not won at the end of this book.  Portland isn't even terribly important to many of the members of the Resistance.  It's not really a major city and only Lena, Alex, and Anabel came from there.<br /><br />There were a lot of things that took up a lot of pages and didn't seem too important.  The whole part with breaking the dam just seemed like a long drawn out way to expose Lu as a traitor and for our Invalids to find out about the troops who were coming.  It really didn't add much to the story or make a big impact on the Resistance (they failed in the end!).  There was also a lot of random conversations between Lena and her mom that didn't really add a whole lot to the story either.  The first half there was a lot of moving about with very little action and then very little moving and suddenly an attack on Portland.<br /><br />Lena spends a lot of time sleeping or by herself and not really interacting with anyone.  She starts to share Julian's tent, but then acts like she doesn't care about him.  She pines for Alex, but not in a way that you really feel her pain.  It is just kind of in a passing way.  She seems to have lost all of the passion she had in the previous books.  During the attack on Portland she doesn't even participate.  She only goes to find Grace.<br /><br />As I got closer to the ending I was starting to think the author would need another book to tie off all these loose ends and give the series the ending it deserved.  This one just doesn't stack up against the first two.  Something about it makes it hard to love these characters anymore.  I guess I just don't understand the point of writing a story as a trilogy if you are going to gloss over so much and not tie everything up at the end.  The whole point is having enough pages to tell the WHOLE story.  I am sad that this book was not all it could have been.assion she had in the previous books. I was taught how to use a traditional wetstone to sharpen knives with and always thought that there should be an easier way. It can be hard to get the correct sharpening angle by hand and each stroke on the stone that is too steep or too shallow only serves to make an already dull blade worse.<br />  Other sharpening options are either heavy and expensive (electric sharpeners) or cheap and ineffective (little hand sharpeners or blade guide systems). With this in mind I thought that this sharpener would be cheap and unable to do the job. I was wrong. It does a wonderful job of sharpening most normal to thin bladed knives even though at first it appears to be nothing more than any other POS plastic and ceramic sharpener. It is able to reset an edge on highly worn pocket knives in just a dozen passes and restores the razor sharp edge to any tactical clip folder in just a few strokes. Even better the fold out diamond rod is very easy to use to sharpen serrated edges. It can even be used to repair some deep nicks to blades from abuse (not that I would ever abuse a blade but skulls are thick). I have even used it to sharpen a K-Bar Tonto [[ASIN:B000BT1G6W Ka-Bar 2-1245-1 Black Tanto Knife]].<br />  To make a long review short, I keep one in my Bag of Evil along with the two leagal length for daily carry knives it would sharpen. B000FCJZ4K|5.0|A friend recommended this book for her book club. I've never been good at networking and figured why not learn as much as I could. This book was perfect for the &#34;beginner&#34; networker who has no idea where to start. Several of Keith's points resonated with me.<br /><br />Point one: help other people! There's an old expression I've heard that you should be good to the people on your way to the top because they're the same people you'll pass on your way back down. Truer words have never been spoken. I've learned the hard way that when it comes to getting a job or a client, it has nothing to do with what you know but WHO you know. Keith's book addresses that issue. He points out that you should help other people in any way you can even if there's nothing in it for you. If you have two friends who could use each other's help, introduce them to each other. If an acquaintance approaches you and asks if you know of anyone hiring, call up your contacts and see how you can be of help. Yes, you may not know that person well but think about what happens if they get the job. They in turn may be able to help you influence a potential client. It's all about paying it forward with significant dividends.<br /><br />Point two: remember people's birthday! Yes, it might sound juvenile but believe it or not, people actually DO care that you remembered their birthday. It touches their soul more than they care to admit. Keith recommended that when you meet someone ask them for their birth month and day (no year necessary). Technology makes it so simple for you to keep track and actually pops up a reminder on that day. You don't necessarily need to buy flowers or anything expensive, a simple card in the mail, email or phone call will do wonders.<br /><br />Point three: timing is everything! After one of Keith's speaking engagements, several people approached him to say thank you or what not. When he got back to his hotel room an email was already waiting for him. It was from one ofthe guys who had approached him after his speech. Keith pointed out that plenty of thoughtful emails had streamed through his inbox over the next couple of days, but he remembers the very first one he received.<br /><br />Those three are only the tip of the iceberg in what is covered in Keith's book. Again, it's honest advice that you can apply tomorrow for use in the real world. You don't have to be rich or influential in order to benefit from Keith's advice, you just have to want to be personable. GET THIS BOOK!!!!

1585104205|5.0|With the available resources this is the friendliest Latin program available.  I have used and looked at other programs but the way this program is meant to be used is superior to all.  The student reads Latin from the start (which is motivating) and uses the deductive methods, working out the meanings of many things, but at the end of each chapter is a summary of the grammar learned in each chapter.  Even though the summary is in Latin (everything is) you can translate it easily.  I have had parents looking at the textbook for the first time able to translate the first chapter just using the clues in the sidebars.  Each chapter advances in grammar which you learn in the story by using it.  It is friendly enough to use with upper elementary students. All Latin study is rigorous but this program takes the \\"tedious\\" out of studying Latin.

B000MCIBE8|5.0|This arrived at the store looking both ominous and inviting, so I had to take it home. Looks were not deceiving. This is a masterpiece. This is a band on a whole other level. I'm still attempting to orient myself as to what precisely this level is, but I have not been able to stop listening to it for over a week. My world shifted perceptively the first time I played it, and I must pursue it relentlessly to discover who I am becoming.<br /> This music has entered me and is crawling around in my head, day and night. This may be the most noticeably altered I have become since I first heard the Beatles. But their's was a music of sunshine and celebration, an opening up to the world. This is the music of darkness; a silky wandering through a dark neon valley where the things they hide from children (and themselves?) are now in open view. (\\"Night divides the day.\\")<br /> Who are these people? The singer is named Jim Morrison, and his dark baritone voice is like the devil whispering into my ear. What is he saying? (\\"Kill! Kill!) The band sounds like it could have played at Hitler and Eva's wedding. It's lush and romantic, but with a dark, sexual allure that covers the ultimate night of death.<br /> The album opens with an invitation: \\"Break on through to the other side.\\" But what is over there, and do I really want to go? There are two long songs. One celebrates love and lust with instruments that spin in a mad whirlwind of ecstasy (\\"Try to set the night on fire!\\") The other is a trip deep into someone's brain that I am afraid might be mine. It's like entering a strange desert or a jungle with a madman for a guide. The music slides in and around while the voice chants either your desires or your nightmares. Perhaps it's both, and then they shift places. The singer ends up killing his father and doing what with his mother? This is disturbing, but equally compelling. How are you supposed to respond to this? (\\"The blue bus is calling us . . .\\")<br /> The short songs are great, most of them about sex (\\"I want to sleep all night in your soul kitchen\\"), dreams (\\"Before you slip into unconsciousness\\"), more dreams (\\"Take the highway to the end of the night\\"), whisky, more sex (\\"I'm your backdoor man\\"), (\\"She's a twentieth century fox\\") and destiny (\\"'Cause it's too late\\"). Some of them are brutal, all of them are beautiful. This is poetry, but it's not Dylan's poetry; it's too haunted for that. This is seduction, but it's not Jagger's; it's mysterious, not cocky.<br /> The band sounds like they are playing hypnotized. There are strange keyboard solos everywhere, the guitar just floats, the drummer is in a daze. Every sound fits perfectly with the words. Just who are these guys? Is everyone in California going insane? I've got to get out there. Raylan and I were listening last night, and he agreed to definitely drive us this summer. Yes, there's something happening, and I don't know what it is. But I've got to find out. I want to \\"speak in secret alphabets.\\"<br /> I lie by the fire in the den, these songs swirling around again before I go to bed. I have to keep the volume low - mom and dad can't hear this, or they'll take it away from me. No, they can't take it away . . . \\"Cause it's too late.\\"<br /><br /> P.G., age 17<br /> January 1967

B004J4WNL2|5.0|This book is a \\"must-read\\" not only for introverts, but also for teachers, parents, managers, church leaders, spouses, and anyone else who deals with different personality types.  We live in a culture that values extroverts over introverts.  This book makes the case that introverts are every bit as valuable as extroverts.  It examines the latest scientific studies that show that a fair amount of our personalities is genetic.  Being introverted isn't a pathology that needs to be cured with psychotherapy.  Nor does being an introvert mean one is a loner or social outcast.  Many introverts have deep friendships and have been very successful in their careers, including attaining prominent leadership positions.  The author makes the case that many introverts are able to adopt some extrovert attributes when necessary to accomplish work they care deeply about, although it comes at a psychic expense and they will need downtime to recharge.<br /><br />This book gives very practical advice for raising introverted children, teaching introverted students, managing both introverted and extroverted employees, how introvert/extrovert couples can work out their differences, and how an introvert can succeed in a culture that places such a high value on being an extrovert.  It's a mix of scientific theory, cultural history, and practical advice.  It's written in a very personal and down-to-earth style.  The author mixes in her own personal experiences along with the personal experiences of others. She visits bastions of extroverted behavior such as Harvard Business School, a large evangelical church, and an elementary school classroom.  She also explores cultures that place a higher value on introverts.<br /><br />As an introvert myself, I found this book very affirming.

Noble<br /><br />This movie is touted as \\"the feel good movie of the year.\\" Anybody who watched this movie and left feeling good about it, in my opinion, is lacking in something vital to their social conscience.<br /><br />I don't know what the creators' intentions were with this film but I will certainly not be planning my next getaway vacation to India.<br /><br />After watching this movie, I am left feeling much as I did after watching the movie, \\"Midnight Express.\\" I was happy for the man's release from the Turkish prison in Midnight Express, just as I am happy for the two slumdogs who are the protagonists in this movie.<br /><br />I definitely think this movie warrants praise and it deserves any awards that have been accorded to it but it certainly didn't make me feel good. It made me feel very, very bad.<br /><br />A slumdog appears to be India's modern day equivalent to what was once referred to in that culture as \\"Untouchables.\\" The word \\"slumdog\\" is, of course, meant to be derogatory. An English equivalent might be dirtbag or scumbag.<br /><br />India once suffered under what was called a caste system and it appears from this movie that they still do.<br /><br />Supposedly this caste system belief is a part of the Hindu religion and its notion of reincarnation and Karma. It is a system of social stratification and a person's karma,<br />his behavior in his previous lives, determines his position in his present life.<br /><br />The karma of certain people is so bad that they are placed in a caste not included in even the lowest caste of the caste system.<br /><br />They are the Untouchables or the Dolits.<br /><br />I remember reading about the horrid lives of these poor Untouchables decades ago.<br />I thought Dickens' \\"Oliver Twist\\" was a horror story until I began reading about the real lives of the Untouchables in the non-fiction Indian culture.<br /><br />These people lived in garbage dumps and scavenged and begged for their survival. The parents in these families would cripple, maim and blind their children so as to make them better beggars and increase their beggary dividends.<br /><br />If I can believe this movie, it does not appear that much has changed in the Indian culture of today. I think it was George Orwell who stated, in his trekking India travelogue, that India would never become a great country because of its Hindu beliefs and the Hindu acceptance of fate and destiny and consequently poverty and social injustice and inequality. Considering the impressions left by this movie, I think he makes a good point.<br /><br />This movie undermines all that I have been reading lately about the new India and the social progress it has been making over the past few decades.<br /><br />That there are people living in India today who are boasting that they will soon be the leading economic culture in the world boggles my imagination. Much worse than that is the notion that if this actually were to be the case, the picture of the future that this film paints for the world is bleak indeed.<br /><br />On the surface this is a love story. But unfortunately the moral, only revealed at the conclusion of the movie is one from that old time religion based on the Hindu notion of karma, destiny and fate.<br /><br />So it seems that the Hindu caste system and the faith that spawned it are still alive and well in modern day India - at least according to this movie.<br /><br />The male hero's mother is murdered as she washes her clothes in some filthy pool of water. Her murder is not explained other than it had something to do with rivaling religious fanatics.<br /><br />The two sons are now orphaned slumdogs. They are captured by a Faganesque, drug dealing gangster. He tortures, maims and brutalizes his captives to keep them in line and enhance their beggary. The two brothers and the younger brother's little girlfriend attempt an escape before the younger boyis blinded by his tortures.<br /><br />The boys escape but the little girl does not catch the train and remains with the drug dealing gangster and his gang of cutthroats. The remainder of this tale centers on this couple's reunification.<br /><br />Somehow mixed in here in this slum ridden, horror show of a country, a modern day TV program is incorporated into the film. As hard as it is to believe, the younger brother has matured and is a contestant on the TV show, an Indian version of \\"Who wants to be a millionaire.\\"<br /><br />The show ends in a Michael Jackson type dance celebration and production extravaganza.<br /><br />As I said in the beginning of this review, I do not know the real intentions of the creators of this film but they left a huge impression on me.<br /><br />Unfortunately that impression is extremely negative.<br /><br />At one point in the movie, one of the characters says to somebody,<br />\\"Well, you wanted to see a true picture of the real India, didn't you?\\"<br /><br />My god! Thank-you very much but I've seen enough of the \\"real\\" India to last me a good while.<br /><br />The Hobo Philosopher is a writer and the author of \\"Hooked on Books\\" among others.is blinded by his tortures.<br /><br />The boys escape but the little girl does not catch the train and remains with the drug dealing gangster and his gang of cutthroats. B005LAIII0|4.0|I was assigned to read Yann Martel's LIFE OF PI in a college freshman lit class over Thanksgiving break.  I had the misfortune to fall sick that Monday, and was bedridden until Friday.  Martel's novel kept me company; in between bouts of passing out, I'd read about the epic journey of young Pi--a journey both physical and spiritual.  I loved the novel; after six years of college literary courses, it remains the only book I've been assigned that I subsequently went out and purchased, and reread, before the course was over.<br /><br />I never expected, or wanted, to see a film version.  Like many great books, PI is rooted in its literary origins.  And as such, the film version does suffer slightly--what comes off as exciting and insightful in the novel can seem somewhat boring and lackluster (at least, plot-wise) onscreen.  This is only to be expected.  And yet, David Magee's script sticks faithfully with the novel, right down to much of the dialogue.  In the form of our tortured hero--I use the word lightly; \\"protagonist\\" would be a better term--we have Suraj Sharma, giving a solid performance (the fantastic Irrfan Khan plays the older Pi, with Rafe Spall playing the Writer).  The problem with the film, aside from the slow pace (which isn't nearly so slow in book form) is that Sharma is out-acted by everything around him; that's only to be expected when you have a film that looks so absolutely beautiful.  WIth vibrant colors, rich textures, and just an overal REALNESS to the cinematography, PI blew me away (and I only watched the 2D version).<br /><br />But the true star here is Richard Parker, Pi's carnivorous raft-mate.  Perhaps the pinnacle of modern CGI (an actual tiger was used for some shots, but mostly it's the computers), Richard Parker comes truly to life under Ang Lee and company's masterful hands.  The fact that a fake tiger is the focal point of LIFE OF PI isn't an accident; it's a beautiful film with a dangerous edge, easily mistaken as a life-affirming, optimistic piece.  Truthfully, the film follows the novel's spirit as well as plot: you take from it what you bring to it.  Maybe its ambiguity in such matters can be counted against it; if the movie (and novel) had taken a true stand, there might be more to admire.  Still, LIFE OF PI--whichever form you approach it in, though I highly recommend the novel, even if Martel's other works fall short--is a story to make you think.  Maybe you'll walk away happy, maybe you'll walk away depressed, maybe you'll just be frustrated and confused; the point is, you'll walk away feeling SOMETHING.  And if that's not the point of art, then I'm not sure what is.optimistic piece.  Truthfully, the film follows the novel's spirit as well as plot: you take from it what you bring to it. Maybe you'll walk away happy, maybe you'll walk away depressed, maybe you'll just be frustrated and confused; the point is, you'll walk away feeling SOMETHING.  And if that's not the point of art, then I'm not sure what is.

The film forgets to insist on the fact the CIA had heavily helped in the coup d'&eacute;tat that ousted the elected Prime Minister. That would have shown how there was a time before 1975 and another after 1975. Before 1975 the US played the cop of the world and after the defeat in Vietnam they became more humble, though not that long. Reagan brought the pride out again. That's what is more or less forgotten in the film that pretends to speak of real events.<br /><br />The film though is quite good at showing the enormous change that started after 1975. In twenty years the world was to be completely transformed. The defeat in Vietnam caused the emergence of strong movements negating imperialism. Iran was one of these though it was hijacked by the hyper fundamentalist clerics of Iranian Islam who imposed a violent revolution with all kinds of coercion against the people who resisted the strict application of Islamic laws, and of course against Americans.<br /><br />The strangest evolution was still to come yet since all those who stuck to a non-market economy were to fall down the historical chute, the flushing chute. The Soviet Union and all their Eastern European block were the first victims and they just fell into the free market economy with a great splash in the world's attentive tension. China felt the trend and changed their policy at the end of the 1970s and we know what happened next. The world was to be completely restructured and economic power was to be completely reshuffled and redistributed.<br /><br />That's in these transitory years of 1979 to 1981 that the hostage crisis in Iran took place. The US embassy was taken over by the revolutionaries and the American personnel was taken hostage, but six had escaped and found refuge at the Canadian Ambassador's residence. The film concerns these six who were in great danger. They were retrieved by a CIA agent sent there as a film producer with the complicity and help of some Hollywood people. That's another change taking place in the USA this time: Americans were ready to serve, even if it was not exactly very good for their public images. Hollywood was on the patriotic side after having been widely against the Vietnam war.<br /><br />That's what I like in this period and in this film. 0062014536|3.0|** SPOILERS OBVIOUSLY **<br /><br />I like stories that have an open ending.  I like when the author doesn't tie everything up in a little bow but still leaves the characters and the story firmly on a particular path so you can draw your own assumptions.  Not everything has to be spelled out.  Not every story has to take the tale all the way to the happily ever after.  That being said, this book did not have one of those endings.  I'd say this book had no ending at all -- the story just stopped, and then out of nowhere we get an epilogue about walls.  If Ms. Oliver was going for an open ending, I'd say she did not succeed.  The end was not written that way.  It was written as though she had a real ending in mind, but her editor told her the book was too long and she had to condense it; or she was an hour from deadline and didn't finish the story yet, so she had to rush to get the book finished to get to the publishing house for review.  I don't mind that we don't know everything that happened to everyone -- I don't mind that we don't see Lena and *spoiler* start their official happily ever after; I don't mind that we don't see how Lena breaks it to *spoiler* that she is choosing the other guy; I don't mind that we don't see what happened with the revolution (they never implied that the entire war hinged on the battle in Portland, so we wouldn't get a resolution there anyway regardless of what happened in this battle).  But you have to lead the characters to the conclusions if you're not going to spell it out!  You have to have some kind of ending even if you don't fill in all the blanks.  There has to be some kind of closure to know that, even if we don't see it for ourselves, A, B and C will happen.  This story had no closure at all.  And I was very disappointed at that.<br /><br />The rest of the book is engaging.  I liked having the perspective of Hana -- we get to learn exactly how it is to be cured.  Lena's continued adventures with the Resistance, and the turmoil of the love triangle -- which wasn't as annoyingly triangle-y as most stories -- provided some good drama.  The awesomeness of some of these scenes balances out the zero star rating I would give for the ending alone.  I'm not disappointed that I bought the book, but I've got to say that it was not a worthy ending to an otherwise fine dystopian series.oil of the love triangle -- which wasn't as annoyingly triangle-y as most stories -- provided some good drama. What about Desmond Child?<br /><br />Although I am a fan of Bon Jovi, I can still claim that, to me, their best days were in the eighties when Desmond Child, the prolific and legendary songwriter, was almost a part of the band. \\"You Give Love A Bad Name\\", \\"Living On A Prayer\\", \\"Bad Medecine\\", \\"Born To Be My Baby\\" became rock anthems and \\"I'd Die For You\\", \\"Without Love\\" and \\"Wild Is The Wind\\" became some of the fan's favorite gems.  All to the credit of Desmond Child. But then, for some obscure reasons, the band decided to let go of their hit maker and went with a different sound. Desmond Child always remained part of the band, with songs here and there, but the quality was never the same. Certainly not because he wasn't able to write hits anymore (he still does it with other bands to this day) but, I suppose, because Bon Jovi told him not to. From the nineties up to now, my critic of any Bon Jovi album has always been the same. Here it is again for \\"What About Now?\\".<br /><br />Everybody who knows about Bon Jovi knows that the band always composes about thirty to forty songs (I exaggerate a bit...) for each album. They then chose about a dozen for a particular CD. Out of those dozens, there are always at least five or six songs that are pure gems. And out of those five or six songs, there are for sure three songs that can be put directly into a greatest hits album. Before, in the eighties, they would do the same thing but you could also add three to four hits by Desmond Child. They would then need to only choose about six songs out of the bunch they had composed for a particular CD.  Since the nineties, for each CD they make, they choose not six songs but a dozen... without any hits by Desmond Child.  Their CDs are, of course, pretty good but they lack genuine big hits. Rock anthems (with the exception of \\"It's My Life\\").  Since \\"These Days\\" (1995), every CD contains one or two songs only by Desmond Child and they do not compare from the songs madein the eighties (\\"Something For The Pain\\", \\"This Ain't A Love Song\\", \\"Real Life\\", \\"One Wild Night\\", \\"The Distance\\", \\"All About Loving You\\", \\"Bells Of Freedom\\", \\"(You Want To) Make A Memory\\", \\"Broken Promiseland\\" and... now... \\"Army Of One\\"?). But take away at least four other songs out of the other seven or nine and replace them with four genuine big rock anthems by Desmond Child (not \\"Army Of One\\", what the hell?)... and you've got yourself one memorable CD.  But without those four big hits, you are left with the same kind of CD Bon Jovi has made since \\"These Days\\"... which is good but not great.<br /><br />And I know that Jon still likes the stuff Desmond Child wrote in the eighties. He copied it with \\"It's My Life\\" and \\"This Is Love, This Is Life\\". Why not go directly at the source (Desmond Child) and write another \\"You Give Love A Bad Name\\" or \\"Living On A Prayer\\"? With a touch of modernity? Wouldn't it make this last CD sound even more cool? And it wouldn't necessarily be a \\"copy-paste\\" of past hits. It would just be Bon Jovi being Bon Jovi and not forget what made them unique in the first place. To this day, every song Desmond Child wrote in the eighties is what makes the crowd go crazy in all the shows Bon Jovi gives.<br /><br />But what the heck. I still love the band. I still buy the CDs. I still sing the new songs. I still do my compilations. And I will definitely sing the five songs I've mentioned before from this last CD. One thing I liked was that with the song \\"What About Now?\\", they kind of made a wink at another old song they had made but that never made it on any particular album. That song was called \\"Cadillac Man\\" and it was never even used in the movie of the same name. It'sone of my favorite demo from them.  I could never comprehend why they never put it on the box set of demos they made a couple of years ago. Anyhow, at least, they kind of acknowledged it with the song \\"What About Now?\\", even if it's on a subconscious level.<br /><br />So there you have it. Old fans won't be disappointed. New fans either. But if you weren't a fan before, you won't be now. It's what Bon Jovi does since 1995. Since they turned away from that Desmond Child touch.

The author claims that a) you will lose weight and b) it will dramatically improve your long term health.<br /><br />Let me address the first part.  I have been trying the diet for a couple of weeks and it has been working for me.  I am one of those people that has that stubborn last 10-15 pounds to lose and I have made more progress, down about 8 lbs since trying the diet.  I find the diet really easy to follow, basically 2 days a week I watch what I eat and keep to the 600 calories.  On those days, I drink more coffee and tea then usual to keep myself from eating, but once resigned to a fast day, basically you just have to make it through the day.  I pick Mondays because they stink anyhow and one other day.  Oddly, once you get used to it, the fasting is really no big deal.  I get hungry a couple times a day and it is amazing a cup of green tea and the hunger goes away.  While 600 calories is not a lot, it is enough to function, work and workout without any difficulties.<br /><br />I do truly believe that this plan will work for me long term as I consider it more of a strategy then a diet. (The strategy concept was in the book.)  Basically, I need to cut my calories down and was not very good at knocking off 500 calories a day for a week by eating slightly less.  So this I think will work for me.  (I will circle back in a month with a weight update.)<br /><br />Moving to the book.  I have two issues with the book. Heck I just explained it in 2 sentences.  Dieting is not rocket science, people need to eat less and burn more to lose weight.  This is one simple strategy for eating less.<br /><br />My second complaint about the book is that it has all sorts of medical claims.  The reality is that the diet is too new and the claims are not supported by hard scientific evidence in my view.  The book is full of anecdotal evidence from people who have been on the diet and also from some studies about fasting.  Not what I call hard science.  I have a lot of trouble believing all the fantastic medical claims that the book put forward.  I am not saying that they are not true, but what I am saying is it is too early and there is too little data.<br /><br />In conclusion, I just don't see any point to the book. 0307352153|5.0|Which people do we value in society? Which people do we acclaim in schools? What skills promise a life of value and success, and how has that changed over time? According to Susan Cain, about a third of Americans are \\"quiet.\\" We are introverts--the buzz and excitement of a party may not terrorize us, but it does leave us drained. We go home to recover when the show's over, while our extrovert friends, enlivened by all the excitement, go on to party elsewhere. And we can succeed, but in a world, or at least a country, that doesn't value our quietness, we struggle to respect our own strengths instead of concentrating on our weaknesses.<br />Have you ever wondered why so much of a student's score in class comes from group projects and classroom interaction? What about the good old days when grades depended on measurable learning? If you're like me, you've certainly known students who get straight As on their homework but score a C in the class, because they're \\"shy.\\" Have you ever wondered why we waste so much time adoring the stars of Hollywood, or demanding our politicians shine like rock stars? Or have you stopped to think how the Warren Buffets and Steve Jobs of our world are actually a) hugely successful because they're b) supremely intelligent introverts?<br />Author Susan Cain goes on a journey not just through the psychology books, but also through Harvard Business School, a super-hyped up Tony Robbins Experience, even Saddleback Evangelical church (in a section entitled Does God Love Introverts), and the internet. Surprisingly, the internet, with its lack of boundaries and fully invaded privacy, presents the place where introverts thrive just as much as extroverts, and the instinct to stop and think before responding is a recognizable advantage.<br />Broken marriages, lost jobs, lonely children, or relationships mended, freedom and success, children who find their way after all, all illustrate the theme. In the end the author reminds us \\"love is essential; gregariousness is optional.\\" We don't all have to live in our comfort zones, but we might need to retreat to them once in a while. 1451620829|5.0|You wanted the best and you got the best! The hottest autobiography in the world: CRISS!!! Well, maybe I'm exaggerating, but if I live 100,000 years, I don't think I'll read a more exciting and candid R&R memoir. It is far and away the best of the ones written by the 3 Kiss members thus far (Paul, what are you waiting for?) and perhaps second only to Chris Lendt's Kiss and Sell as the \\"best Kiss book ever\\" (sorry Gordon Gebert, I loved Kiss and Tell, but I prefer more cerebral fodder--hey, that's why I'm a Kiss fan!). But on to the book. Peter Criss narrates his rough beginnings as a young Hooligan from Brooklyn, but quickly gets to the good stuff: his life as a musician, and as Kiss' definitive drummer. Certainly the Kiss years are the most exciting part of the story, and Peter dishes a lot of dirt and previously unknown facts about the members of Kiss. By the time Peter joined Kiss, he had Nothin' To Lose, as he had already been in many failed bands, and his Rock and Roll biological clock was ticking. But once he joins Kiss, things get Hotter than Hell pretty quickly for him and the boys with whom he'll be playing all night, much to the chagrin of his first wife Beth (Sorry! I mean Lydia, who gets revenge against the Two Timer with her own tell-all book later!). Life on the road is typical rock band stuff--a lot of Room Service, going All the Way with Ladies in Waiting, and drinking Cold Gin, among other chemical inducements (cocaine was Peter's main addiction). Unfortunately Peter's Strange Ways soon become a detriment to the teetotalers Gene and Paul (whom he lambastes with gusto throughout the book). By 1980, the whole Kiss thing is more or less dead, and Peter is quietly tossed from the group, though he gets 25% of the take for a few years, allowing him to live like a Parasite off his ex-bandmates' earnings. But soon the revamped Kiss's fortunes slow down to a drip during their rotating-lead-guitarist-period, and Peter has to get back to work, lest he fall Into the Void. His solo career stalls, however, as it usually does for most members of successful rock bands who try the solo route (he blames Gene and Paul for this, of course!). And his Dirty Livin' and Rock and Rolling all night soon take their toll. He gets remarried to another Hard Luck Woman, Debra, who is as big a cokehead as Peter, and their Great Expectations (and even greater sex) soon Peter out (ouch!), with our drummer ending up almost broke and alone, barely surviving the San Francisco earthquake of the 90s. Peter eventually hits Rock Bottom and ends up doing a stint in rehab, which he completes successfully, much to his credit. And in the tradition of Behind the Music, everything turns out OK in the end. The original Kiss lineup reunites for a few years, the Psycho Circus gets back on the road, Rocking in the USA (and worldwide), and although Peter is paid journeyman wages for the gig (and inevitably gets fired yet again), the revival gets him out of the financial hole. Soon it's Kissin' Time again, this time with Gigi, who becomes his third wife, and clearly the best of the lot (third is indeed the charm here!). Peter then scores a successful acting gig on Oz, and continues to produce solo material, and of course this book. His story is Larger Than Life, and much better written then the usual rock star auto-hagiographies--for example, Gene used his books mainly to self promote, and Ace seemed to remember very little about his Kiss years (Paul, come on, Man, we are waiting for your version of Rashomon!).  Having read almost everything by and about Kiss, there was little here to Shock Me (except for a couple of truly disgusting anecdotes about Gene's hygiene and sexual practices), but overall Mr. Criss deserves much credit for this opus. He is at times whiny and tends to externalize his problems, but he does speak candidly about his own flaws. Despite the animosity Peter holds toward his ex-bandmates (even Ace), all 4 band members and associates are presented in a balanced manner, warts and all, with good points as well as bad points--indeed, they seem Almost Human. Peter is a man of passion and conviction, and his book is worth a Deuce (or at least the $15.47 price offered on Amazon). Makeup to Breakup is a real Rocket Ride for the Kiss fan, a Black Diamond for sure. Shout it out loud!

B005HP2JAM|2.0|I'm less inclined to comment on the content of Herzog's documentary.  I'm generally a fan of what he does, and I think this film, like his others, does a good job of presenting its subject in a way that is honest and straightforward, but with with an artfully constructed narrative.<br /><br />What I do feel inclined to comment on was the 3D cinematography.  I am absolutely a fan of 3D cinema, especially when it's used for a good cause.  In so many ways, this was a perfect concept for a 3D film, and at certain moments I was extremely appreciative that the film was shot in 3D, as you can really experience the shape of the cave for yourself, and see details you would otherwise miss.<br /><br />Unfortunately, the depth composition in the shots is all over the map.  I have a home projector, so I was watching on about a 100\\" screen.  Some of the shots were pretty unwatchable due to divergence of the far plane (when the two images at the farthest depth are farther apart than a set of human eyes).  There were also quite a few shots that had been very VERY badly converted from 2D.  If you closed your left eye, you could see where the right eye's image had halos of material sliding around with foreground objects.  I have absolutely no problem with stereoscopic conversion, but it must be done right.  I'm curious which company did the work on this film, as it was nothing like the high end work I've come to expect from StereoD or Legend3D.  Just absolute garbage that really doesn't do the film justice.<br /><br />Many shots throughout the film were shot with too much depth.  It seems as if when they shot it they were setting the inter-axial distance (distance between cameras, which determines the amount of depth in the shot) based on their on set monitors (which may have been extremely small, since they were bringing them into a cave).  Unfortunately, as you show content on bigger and bigger screens, the far depths start to pull your eyes farther and farther apart until they bleed.  Such was the case with Cave of Forgotten Dreams.<br /><br />I really hate to rag on a documentary for having poor stereoscopic cinematography, as I know it's extremely expensive and time-consuming to do well, and they obviously just didn't have the money to do it properly.  Despite that, I will say that I STILL found the 3D viewing experience more engaging than the 2D one.  I think that's a testimony to how good of an idea it was to do this film in 3D.  It's just too bad it didn't turn out better.  The good news is that they could probably go back and reconvert a lot of these shots.  The ones with leafy foliage would be a little tough, but the caves are relatively straightforward.  I'd be surprised if they actually did this, but who knows?  I still can't believe those loose mattes...  How did that get through QC?

It is now regarded as one of Eric's finest. Well done by a true master.  It has been a wonderful 50 years of Eric Clapton contributing to the soundtrack of many of our lives.

0062014536|4.0|This book starts fairly quickly after the second book in the series, Pandemonium, ends. Waiting that year was so painful I actually re-read Pandemonium to get myself back to the right frame of mind to read this.<br /><br />Requiem, much like the two previous books, sucked me in right away to the point that I couldn't put the book down. Sleep and work became very hard while reading this. This book alternates points of view between Lena, our main heart of the story, and Hana, Lena's once BFF. Lena is back with the resistance and this time with Julian. She is having a hard time with finding out that Alex isn't dead, but very much alive. How Oliver handles the feelings between the three is very interesting to me. Julian is confused because everything he knows isn't `right' anymore. Alex is confused how Lena could fall in love with someone else and Lena is confused with how Alex is alive and she's now in love with Julian. I found the situation to be handled quite realistically for what could be considered realistic in a dystopian novel. I have read reviews where the reader was upset because one of the three didn't do the `obvious' choice. The obvious choices are often the most overlooked choices and while Oliver painted a heartbreaking portrayal of love, she also painted a relatable one.<br /><br />Lena is also fighting other forces in her life: her mother being alive, there being a traitor in her group and fighting their way back into the city.<br /><br />Hana's life is very different from Lena's. She is `happy.' She is living what many would consider to be a picture perfect happy life and is on her way to marriage. The closer she gets to marriage, though, the more Hana realizes not everything is perfect with her match, and maybe the cure didn't work the way it was supposed to.<br /><br />There is that pivotal perfect point where everything converges and the big BANG occurs and though it could be considered perfect timing (and it is), the book also ends very open-ended but with a strong message that was carried throughout the novel.<br /><br />Oh this book. This series has stolen my heart from the moment I started the first book, Delirium. This is one of those series I shouldn't have started until the last book came out. I've spent the past two years moaning about this book series to my friend and how it killed me when I read it. That being said, Requiem didn't let me down. At all. Even the stuff I didn't agree with didn't bother me because Oliver's writing is so strong and beautiful. If anything it protected me like a blanket when I wanted to cry.<br /><br />In my opinion, it is a beautiful ending to the series. Oliver could have taken it many different ways, and in a way, she did by having two different alternating narrators, but it still worked. If anything, Hana, who I spent most of the series ambivalent towards, I ended up liking because I got her side of the story, even if I didn't agree with everything that she did. That is how good Oliver's writing is, even when I disagreed with a character, I understood why she went there. Some moments it was uncomfortable and painful, but they were always needed.<br /><br />The end to this series isn't going to make everyone happy, and it was fairly open-ended to the point that I want to know more, but I'm still content with this being the last book. Which seems to be a rare thing from fellow reviewers, and I find hard to believe because I know I had high expectations from this story.<br /><br />Originally posted at yAdultReview.but with a strong message that was carried throughout the novel.<br /><br />Oh this book. This series has stolen my heart from the moment I started the first book, Delirium. This is one of those series I shouldn't have started until the last book came out. I've spent the past two years moaning about this book series to my friend and how it killed me when I read it. That being said, Requiem didn't let me down. At all. Even the stuff I didn't agree with didn't bother me because Oliver's writing is so strong and beautiful. If anything it protected me like a blanket when I wanted to cry.<br /><br />In my opinion, it is a beautiful ending to the series. Oliver could have taken it many different ways, and in a way, she did by having two different alternating narrators, but it still worked. If anything, Hana, who I spent most of the series ambivalent towards, I ended up liking because I got her side of the story, even if I didn't agree with everything that she did. That is how good Oliver's writing is, even when I disagreed with a character, I understood why she went there. Some moments it was uncomfortable and painful, but they were always needed.<br /><br />The end to this series isn't going to make everyone happy, and it was fairly open-ended to the point that I want to know more, but I'm still content with this being the last book. Which seems to be a rare thing from fellow reviewers, and I find hard to believe because I know I had high expectations from this story.<br /><br />Originally posted at yAdultReview.

Better the mirror held up to see the reflection of a lifetime of choices.<br /><br />The atheist and the person of faith share the same conundrum: neither can speak rationally about the unknowable or metaphysical. But what we experience as \\"real\\" depends on how we tell the story?  And that's where we find God?  Too thin for me.  I do appreciate the author and director for tackling this question, however.
